# Apple Inc. timeline

Discover Apple’s journey over the years with the help of our downloadable free Apple history timeline.

[![Tim Stumbles photo](https://img.officetimeline.com/uploads/assets/main_Tim_Stumbles_52ab010f7b.webp)\\
\\
Tim Stumbles](https://www.officetimeline.com/blog/author/tim-stumbles)

Mar 29, 2018

14 min read

_Last updated on March 13, 2024_

Dive into the rich heritage of Apple Inc. with our detailed **Apple history timeline,** carefully crafted using [Office Timeline](http://www.officetimeline.com/), an intuitive PowerPoint add-in designed for creating visually impactful timelines. This blog post will guide you through the pivotal moments that have shaped Apple’s innovative journey, offering a glimpse into the milestones that have defined the tech giant’s path to success.

![Timeline of Apple Inc.](https://img.officetimeline.com/uploads/assets/main_apple_history_timeline_3240bb0a4c.webp)

[Download free PowerPoint file](https://img.officetimeline.com/uploads/assets/apple_history_timeline_be40e18f6c.pptx)

Apple Inc. was born on the **1st of April 1976** and was founded by **Steve Jobs**, **Steve Wozniak**, and **Ronald Wayne**. The company’s first product was the Apple I, a personal computer designed and hand-built entirely by Wozniak.

Gradually expanding its range of products to consumer electronics and online services, it took the pioneering venture less than half a century to become [the world’s largest smartphone manufacturer](https://www.statista.com/statistics/271496/global-market-share-held-by-smartphone-vendors-since-4th-quarter-2009/#:~:text=Apple%20held%20the%20largest%20slice,the%20fourth%20quarter%20of%202023).

## **Apple history timeline: key milestones**

The Apple Inc. Timeline overviews the important events in the history of the business, including releases of their main products and services. Let’s retrace the major milestones of their journey **since establishment until the present day**:

- **1976**: Apple Computer Company is founded
- **1976**: Apple I
- **1977**: Apple II
- **1980**: Apple III
- **1980**: Company goes public for $22 per share
- **1984**: The Macintosh
- **1985**: S. Jobs leaves the company
- **1989**: The Macintosh Portable
- **1990**: 3 new Macintosh models
- **1991**: The PowerBook
- **1997**: S. Jobs regains position as CEO
- **1997**: Apple Online Store
- **1998**: The iMac
- **2001**: First retail stores
- **2001**: The iPod
- **2003**: iTune Store
- **2007**: Launches the iPhone & Apple TV under new name: Apple Inc.
- **2008**: App Store
- **2010**: The iPad
- **2010**: The iPhone 4
- **2010**: Apple shares reach an all-time high
- **2011**: Jobs resigns (health problems)
- **2012**: The iPhone 5
- **2017**: Clips app
- **2015**: Apple Watch
- **2016**: AirPods
- **2018**: Apple’s value reaches $1 trillion
- **2023**: Apple Vision Pro
- **2026**: 50th anniversary

## **An in-depth look at the milestones in Apple's history**

Let’s take a closer look at how Apple became creator of game-changing devices like the Mac, iPhone and iPod:

#### **1976: Apple Computer Company is founded, and Apple I is born**

The identity behind one of the world’s most profitable brands emerged in the garage of Steve Jobs’ childhood home, after he came up with the idea of selling Steve Wozniak’s hand-built computer **Apple I**. To finance the creation of the device, Jobs sold his VW Kombi van and Wozniak his HP-65 calculator. The prototype was released on the 11th of April 1976, demonstrated 4 months later at the Homebrew Computer Club, and sold at the introductory price of $666 (the equivalent of $2,867 in 2017).

#### **1977: Apple II**

**Apple II** (stylized in the logo as “ **apple \]\[**“)was a pioneering 8-bit home computer introduced in 1977, featuring color graphics, innovative design by Steve Wozniak, and a redesigned Apple logo. It revolutionized the personal computer industry, opening doors to consumer software. Apple II earned acclaim as [the greatest PC of all time by PC World in 2006](https://web.archive.org/web/20080511194506/http:/www.pcworld.com/article/id,126692-page,10-c,systems/article.html#II).\
\
#### **1980: Apple III**\
\
**The Apple III** (stylized as “ **apple ///**”) was the first system designed by Apple as a company. It was a business-focused PC released by Apple in 1980 as a successor to the Apple II series. It was designed to provide business users with features such as a true typewriter-style upper/lowercase keyboard and an 80-column display.\
\
The product incurred massive expenses in research, development, and promotion. Unfortunately, because of significant design flaws, it became Apple’s **first major market failure**. Despite a year-long effort to rectify issues such as chip dislodging, malfunctioning clocks, and overheating, solutions came too late.\
\
#### **1980: Company goes public for $22 per share**\
\
Four years after its foundation, Apple Computer Inc. went public at $22 per share, which generated the **greatest capital that any IPO had done** since Ford Motor Company in 1956, making 300 people millionaires overnight.\
\
#### **1984: The Macintosh**\
\
The original **Apple Macintosh** personal computer, later rebranded as the **Macintosh 128K**, was introduced in 1984. With a graphical user interface, built-in screen, and mouse, this computer helped popularize **desktop publishing** due to its ability to handle graphics. The new machine included the powerful 68k microprocessor from the Apple Lisa. Despite initial hurdles, the Macintosh achieved loyal fans and solid sales figures.\
\
#### **1985: Steve Jobs leaves the company**\
\
Throughout its activity, the company has seen numerous executive job shuffles, with all three co-founders leaving the board in the early stages and only Jobs returning in 1997 to oversee the rebuilding and consolidation of Apple’s status.\
\
#### **1989: The Macintosh Portable**\
\
The **Macintosh Portable**, Apple’s first battery-powered Mac, was sold from 1989 to 1991. It featured a sharp monochrome LCD screen that was housed in a unique clamshell design, allowing the screen to fold shut against the keyboard. This innovative laptop also included an integrated trackball for navigation and came in an array of technical configurations to satisfy various user needs.\
\
While critics acknowledged the vivid quality of its display, many found the bulky machine to be quite heavy and pricey. Sales failed to meet projections, leading Apple to pull the plug on the Portable in 1991 when they launched their new PowerBook line-up of portable Macs that addressed earlier complaints.\
\
#### **1990: 3 new Macintosh models**\
\
In 1990, Apple rolled out a trio of new Macintosh computers, each tailored for a distinct slice of the personal computer marketplace. First came the **Macintosh LC** (Low Cost), a relatively inexpensive option designed for mainstream consumers seeking colorful graphical capabilities on a budget. While affordable and attractive visually, its functionality was constrained.\
\
By contrast, the high-powered **Macintosh IIfx** workstation aimed higher, with blazing speeds and extensive customization choices to satisfy professional power users handling data-intensive tasks.\
\
Between these extremes lay the **Macintosh Classic**, a no-frills machine delivering basic computing skills for essential word processing and spreadsheet duties without fancy flourishes. These three models catered to different segments of the market, providing a range of options for users with varying needs and budgets.\
\
#### **1991: The PowerBook**\
\
The **PowerBook 100** was designed by Sony for Apple Computer. It was unveiled at the 1991 COMDEX computer expo. Despite its small marketing budget, the PowerBook line was a success, generating over $1 billion in revenue for Apple in its debut year alone. It was praised for its unique design, with the PowerBook 100 being named the tenth-greatest PC of all time in 2006.\
\
#### **1997: Steve Jobs regains position as CEO**\
\
In 1997, Steve Jobs returned as CEO of Apple, reviving the company’s fortunes after years of struggle. His comeback followed Apple’s acquisition of NeXT, the company he founded during his time away.\
\
With Jobs back at the helm, Apple underwent a **dramatic transformation**, introducing ground-breaking products like the iMac, iPod, iPhone, and iPad. Jobs’ relentless pursuit of innovation brought Apple to new heights, solidifying its position as a global tech powerhouse. His return marked the beginning of a new era of creativity and dominance for the company.\
\
#### **1997: Apple Online Store**\
\
Once more, 1997 proved a landmark year for Apple. The online **Apple Store** officially opened on November 10, 1997, and has been online since, offering worldwide buyers direct access to coveted gadgets. The online Apple Store played a crucial role in Apple’s survival and resurgence alongside the return of Steve Jobs and the launch of the iMac and iPod.\
\
#### **1998: The iMac**\
\
The **iMac G3**, sold by Apple from 1998 to 2003, released under Steve Jobs’ reorganized Apple, proved a **landmark** in Apple’s history. It featured a distinctive all-in-one design with innovative use of translucent plastic, replacing legacy technologies with CD-ROMs and USB ports.\
\
Despite mixed critical response, the iMac achieved remarkable commercial success, becoming Apple’s **fastest-selling computer** with over 5 million units sold.\
\
#### **2001: First retail stores**\
\
The opening of **Apple stores** in 2001 had a strong impact on technology retailing. With innovative architectural designs, the stores quickly became destinations known for exhibiting Apple’s cutting-edge devices.\
\
As more places launched worldwide, they gained praise for establishing new standards in visual appeal and utility. By combining art and engineering, Apple Stores have transformed business spaces into brand experiences.\
\
#### **2001: The iPod**\
\
The **iPod**, released by Apple in 2001, changed the way people listened to music. It enabled users to carry thousands of songs in a pocket-sized player and to download straight from the internet. It transformed the way music was accessed and experienced. Hewlett-Packard briefly distributed private labeled iPods for a short time, but conflicts over contractual terms brought that partnership to a halt in 2005.\
\
Apple teamed up with U2 in 2006 to deliver a special edition iPod that let fans securely store the band’s entire catalog locally. Both the Nano and Shuffle lines were retired by Apple in July 2017, signaling the end of those product families. On May 10, 2022 the iPod Touch was discontinued, definitively ending the iPod era.\
\
#### **2003: iTune Store**\
\
In 2003, Apple launched the **iTunes Store**, an online digital media store where users could electronically purchase and download music, TV shows, movies, and other media. It was a new concept at the time that proved highly successful.\
\
#### **2007: Launches the iPhone & Apple TV under new name Apple Inc.**\
\
The year 2007 marked important changes for Apple Computers Inc., which became **Apple Inc**.\
\
The corporation introduced the **iPhone** showing that phones could be so much more than just for calls and texts. It was a multifunctional handheld device that allowed users to communicate, access multimedia, and browse the internet from the palm of their hand.\
\
In the same year, Apple launched **Apple TV**, bringing internet video and photos to the family television in a sleek, WIFI-connected package.\
\
#### **2008: App Store**\
\
In 2008, Apple changed the mobile landscape introducing the **App Store** which allowed iPhone users to directly download a diverse array of applications from a digital marketplace curated by Apple.\
\
This original concept enabled third-party developers to create and distribute applications from simple games and social media tools to complex productivity apps. Smartphones become **personalized multi-functional devices**.\
\
#### **2010: The iPad**\
\
Two years later, in 2010, Apple unveiled the **iPad**, a device similar to the iPhone but with a larger screen, optimized for media consumption and digital content creation.\
\
With its expansive touchscreen display, the iPad provided users with an immersive experience for watching videos, reading electronically published books and periodicals, and playing games. Its larger screen made it easier to use applications for high-level designing, photo editing, video conferencing, and other work or fun activities.\
\
#### **2010: The iPhone 4**\
\
In 2010, Apple marked another milestone on the [iPhone timeline](https://www.officetimeline.com/blog/apple-iphone-timeline) by launching the **iPhone 4**, a smartphone that featured a sleek design with glass front and rear panels, high-resolution Retina display and enhanced camera system. The device’s debut caused a frenzy, with over 600,000 pre-orders – the largest number of pre-orders Apple has ever taken in a single day, far higher than anticipated. The iPhone 4 attracted widespread acclaim for its **cutting-edge aesthetics and capabilities**. 2010 was a great year for Apple: they were leading the way in the world of mobile technology.\
\
#### **2010: Apple shares reach an all-time high**\
\
On Wall Street, Apple stock rocketed to an unprecedented $275 per share. This record was achieved as a result of staggering iPhone 4 sales that had prompted the early order process to be briefly suspended due to being **overwhelmed by consumer enthusiasm**.\
\
Also, investor optimism was boosted on anticipation of forthcoming products combined with strong sales and financial reports from existing blockbusters.\
\
#### **2011: Jobs resigns (health problems)**\
\
In 2011, Steve Jobs resigned as CEO of Apple due to health issues. **Tim Cook** took over as Apple CEO in 2011, following Steve Jobs’ death. That same year, Cook introduced the Apple Watch, a new product category for Apple. During the announcement, Cook paid tribute to Jobs by using his famous catchphrase, “one more thing.”\
\
#### **2012: The iPhone 5**\
\
The **iPhone 5** was launched on September 12, 2012. It had a new aluminium design, a taller 4-inch screen, a faster processor, high-speed 4G LTE connectivity, and a retooled 8-megapixel iSight camera. It got over two million pre-orders in a day.\
\
Yet, early adopters encountered some hardware glitches, and the newly adapted Lightning charging port drew polarized opinions and lingering complaints. Apple stopped selling the iPhone 5 on September 10, 2013, when they introduced the iPhone 5S and the iPhone 5C, which replaced it.\
\
#### **2017: Clips app**\
\
Apple introduces **Clips**, a new app for iPhone and iPad. The app was aimed at empowering everyone with creative expression, allowing easy creation of expressive videos on iPhone and iPad.\
\
The video editing app allowed users to liven up short capture with effects, stickers, filters and more through an intuitive interface. Clips became a playground for casual content creators of all ages to craft and share moments with just a few taps.\
\
#### **2015: Apple Watch**\
\
The year 2015 marked a major shift in personal technology as Apple launched the **Apple Watch**, their first wearable. While sleek and stylish in design, the true innovation of the Apple Watch consisted in multiple health and fitness tracking functions.\
\
It allowed users to monitor vitals, exercise routines, and stay connected through notifications and apps from their wrist.\
\
#### **2016: AirPods**\
\
Apple caused a stir by announcing in 2016 plans to remove the traditional headphone jack from iPhones. In its place, they launched **AirPods**, completely wireless earbuds that offered unimpeded music and call experience and became a must-have accessory. Their impressive battery life and seamless pairing made AirPods an instant success story.\
\
#### **2018: Apple’s value reaches $1 trillion**\
\
On September 12th of 2018, Apple made history by becoming the very first publicly traded company to reach an unprecedented market value of **one trillion dollars**. This achievement showed the tremendous success and worldwide influence of Apple’s many products and services. It marked its dominant position within the market on a global scale.\
\
#### **2023: Apple Vision Pro**\
\
In 2023, during the WWDC event in June, Apple CEO Tim Cook unveiled the ground-breaking **Vision Pro** – Apple’s first **mixed-reality headset** and the company’s most important product launch since the revolutionary Apple Watch. It was released on February 2, 2024, and carries a hefty price tag of $3,499.\
\
## **Frequently asked questions about Apple Inc.**\
\
We’ve gone further and researched more about Apple’s history. Here are the answers to frequent questions that people ask about lesser-known, interesting facts regarding the company.\
\
### What does Apple Inc do?\
\
Apple Inc. designs, manufactures, and markets a diverse portfolio of electronic goods, including smartphones, personal computers, tablets, wearables, and accessories; the company also provides a variety of related support services. Its product categories include iPhone, Mac, iPad as well as items in the Wearables, Home, and Accessories segments.\
\
### Why did Apple change to Apple Inc?\
\
During his keynote presentation at Macworld Expo 2007 on January 9th, Steve Jobs announced that Apple Computer, Inc. was adopting the shortened name Apple Inc. to symbolize its evolution beyond computers alone. Apple would no longer focus only on building PCs; rather, it aimed to develop a variety of consumer electronics. The rebranding coincided with unveiling the iPhone and Apple TV. This represented a landmark, when Apple diversified its portfolio to transform how people connect, communicate, and are entertained.\
\
### What is Apple’s slogan?\
\
Apple’s slogan is “Think Different”. After its initial airing on television in 1997, Apple’s “Think Different” slogan remains linked to the corporation today and continues glowing beside their innovative line of products. These words stand for the potential and progressiveness that drive the company forward.\
\
### Why Apple Inc. was named “apple”?\
\
The name “Apple” was chosen due to Steve Jobs’ liking of apples. In [Walter Isaacson’s biography of Steve Jobs](https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11084145-steve-jobs), Jobs reveals that he came up with the name “Apple” while on one of his fruitarian diets.\
\
After visiting an apple farm, Steve thought of the name Apple because “it sounded fun, spirited and not intimidating…plus, it would get us ahead of Atari in the phone book.” As Steve Wozniak remembered, he drove Steve from the airport down Highway 85. Steve had been to Oregon to visit a place he called an “apple orchard”. During their ride, Steve Jobs suggested the name “Apple Computer” for their fledgling business.\
\
Another hypothesis links the name of the company to Isaac Newton since the original logo featured Isaac Newton under an apple tree. Jobs even included a quote from a poem indicating a connection to Newton.\
\
### Why did Steve Jobs choose Apple logo?\
\
Jobs wanted a logo that would represent the importance of design in modern computers, harmonizing it with the “Apple” brand. The result was a two-dimensional rainbow-colored apple image. This new design highlighted Apple’s groundbreaking Apple II – the world’s first colored-display computer.\
\
### Why is Apple logo half eaten?\
\
Rob Janoff, the graphic designer who created the logo in 1977, stated that the bite was primarily added to avoid mistaking the apple for a cherry. The colorful, friendly logo was designed to make computers feel more approachable and less intimidating to newcomers.\
\
### What did Apple do before iPhone?\
\
For over three decades, Apple Computer mainly manufactured personal computers for everyday use, such as the highly successful Apple II, Macintosh, and Power Mac lines. They strived to craft intuitive devices that non-experts could easily operate in homes and workplaces. However, the company faced difficulties throughout the 1990s, such as fluctuating sales and diminished market share.\
\
### Why did Steve Jobs create Apple?\
\
Jobs and co-founder Wozniak dreamt of designing compact computers for mass adoption. The computers would have been user-friendly enough for everyday use in homes and offices. They aspired to make computing more accessible to all.\
\
### At what age did Steve Jobs start Apple?\
\
At only 21 years old, Jobs embarked on his pioneering journey with Wozniak in his family garage. To fund their ambitious startup, Jobs sold his trusty Volkswagen bus, while Wozniak parted with his beloved scientific calculator.\
\

Title: History of Apple Inc. - Wikipedia
# History of Apple Inc.

History of Apple Inc.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Apple_logo_black.svg/500px-Apple_logo_black.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_logo_black.svg) Current [Apple Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Inc. "Apple Inc.") logo, introduced in 1998, discontinued in 2000, and re-established in 2014[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-1)

[Apple Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Inc. "Apple Inc."), originally Apple Computer, Inc., is an American [multinational corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multinational_corporation "Multinational corporation") that creates and markets [consumer electronics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consumer_electronics "Consumer electronics") and attendant computer software, and is a [digital distributor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_distribution "Digital distribution") of media content. Apple's core product lines are the [iPhone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPhone "IPhone") line of [smartphones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smartphone "Smartphone"), the [iPad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPad "IPad") line of [tablet computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tablet_computer "Tablet computer"), and the [Mac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mac_(computer) "Mac (computer)") line of [personal computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Personal_computer "Personal computer"). The company offers [its products](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Apple_products "List of Apple products") online and has a chain of retail stores known as [Apple Stores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Stores "Apple Stores"). Founders [Steve Jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Jobs "Steve Jobs"), [Steve Wozniak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Wozniak "Steve Wozniak"), and [Ronald Wayne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Wayne "Ronald Wayne") created Apple Computer Co. on April 1, 1976, to market Wozniak's [Apple I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_I "Apple I") desktop computer,[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-partnership-2) and Jobs and Wozniak incorporated the company on January 3, 1977,[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Apple_incorporation-3) in [Cupertino, California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupertino,_California "Cupertino, California").

For more than three decades, Apple Computer was predominantly a manufacturer of personal computers, including the [Apple II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_II "Apple II"), Macintosh, and [Power Mac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_Macintosh "Power Macintosh") lines, but it faced rocky sales and low market share during the 1990s. Jobs, who had been ousted from the company in 1985, returned to Apple in 1997 after his company [NeXT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NeXT "NeXT") was bought by Apple.[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-4) The following year he became the company's interim CEO,[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-interim_CEO-5) which later became permanent.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-norr-6) Jobs subsequently instilled a new corporate philosophy of recognizable products and simple design, starting with the [original iMac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IMac_G3 "IMac G3") in 1998.

With the introduction of the successful [iPod](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPod "IPod") music player in 2001 and [iTunes Music Store](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ITunes_Store "ITunes Store") in 2003, Apple established itself as a leader in the consumer electronics and media sales industries, leading it to drop "Computer" from the company's name in 2007. The company is also known for its [iOS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IOS "IOS") range of smartphone, media player, and tablet computer products that began with the iPhone, followed by the [iPod Touch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPod_Touch "IPod Touch") and then iPad. As of June30,2015[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Apple_Inc.&action=edit), Apple was the [largest publicly traded corporation in the world by market capitalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_corporations_by_market_capitalization "List of corporations by market capitalization"),[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-7) with an estimated value of US$1 trillion as of August2,2018[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Apple_Inc.&action=edit).[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-8) Apple's worldwide annual revenue in 2010 totaled US$65 billion, growing to US$127.8 billion in 2011[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-9) and $156 billion in 2012.[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-10-K-2012-10)

## 1971–1985: Jobs and Wozniak

### Pre-foundation

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Blue_Box_in_museum.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Blue_Box_in_museum.jpg) [Steve Wozniak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Wozniak "Steve Wozniak")'s " [blue box](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_box "Blue box")" at the [Computer History Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_History_Museum "Computer History Museum")

[Steve Jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Jobs "Steve Jobs") and [Steve Wozniak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Wozniak "Steve Wozniak"), referred to collectively as "the two Steves", first met in mid-1971, when their mutual friend [Bill Fernandez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Fernandez "Bill Fernandez") introduced then 21-year-old Wozniak to 16-year-old Jobs.[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20041%E2%80%932-11)[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady20091%E2%80%932-12) Their first business partnership began in the fall of that year when Wozniak, a self-educated electronics engineer, read an article in _Esquire_ magazine that described a device that could place free long-distance phone calls by emitting specific tone chirps. Wozniak started to build his original “ [blue boxes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_box "Blue box")”, which he tested by calling the [Vatican City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vatican_City "Vatican City") pretending to be [Henry Kissinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Kissinger "Henry Kissinger") wanting to speak to the pope.[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-13) Jobs managed to sell some two hundred blue boxes for $150 each, and split the profit with Wozniak.[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20041%E2%80%932-11)[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady20091%E2%80%932-12) Jobs later told his biographer that if it hadn't been for Wozniak's blue boxes, "there wouldn't have been an Apple."[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201530-14)

By 1972, Jobs had withdrawn from [Reed College](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reed_College "Reed College") and Wozniak from [UC Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley "University of California, Berkeley"). Wozniak designed a video terminal that he could use to log on to the minicomputers at Call Computer. Alex Kamradt commissioned the design and sold a small number of them through his firm. Aside from their interest in up-to-date technology, the impetus for the two Steves seems to have had another source. In his essay _From Satori to Silicon Valley_ (published 1986), cultural historian [Theodore Roszak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore_Roszak_(scholar) "Theodore Roszak (scholar)") made the point that Apple Computer emerged from within the West Coast [counterculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterculture "Counterculture") and the need to produce print-outs, letter labels, and databases. Roszak offers a bit of background on the development of the two Steves' prototype models.

In 1975, the two Steves started attending meetings of the [Homebrew Computer Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homebrew_Computer_Club "Homebrew Computer Club").[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Apple_Confidential-15) New microcomputers such as the [Altair 8800](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altair_8800 "Altair 8800") and the [IMSAI 8080](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IMSAI_8080 "IMSAI 8080") inspired Wozniak to build a microprocessor into his video terminal circuit to make a complete computer. At the time the only [microcomputer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microcomputer "Microcomputer") [CPUs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_processing_unit "Central processing unit") generally available were the $179 [Intel 8080](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intel_8080 "Intel 8080") (equivalent to $1,071in 2025), and the $170 [Motorola 6800](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motorola_6800 "Motorola 6800") (equivalent to $1,017in 2025). Wozniak preferred the 6800, but both were out of his price range. So he watched, and learned, and designed computers on paper, waiting for the day he could afford a CPU.

When [MOS Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MOS_Technology "MOS Technology") released its $20 (equivalent to $113in 2025) [6502](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MOS_Technology_6502 "MOS Technology 6502") chip in 1976, Wozniak wrote a version of [BASIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BASIC "BASIC") for it, then began to design a computer for it to run on. The 6502 was designed by the same people who designed the 6800, as many in Silicon Valley left employers to form their own companies. Wozniak's earlier 6800 paper-computer needed only minor changes to run on the new chip.

By March 1, 1976, Wozniak completed the machine and took it to a [Homebrew Computer Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homebrew_Computer_Club "Homebrew Computer Club") meeting to show it off.[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20045-16) When Jobs saw Wozniak's computer, which later became the [Apple I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_I "Apple I"), he was immediately interested in its commercial potential.[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-17) Initially, Wozniak intended to share schematics of the machine for free, but Jobs insisted that they should instead build and sell bare [printed circuit boards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Printed_circuit_board "Printed circuit board") for the computer.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201562-18) Wozniak originally offered the design to [Hewlett-Packard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hewlett-Packard "Hewlett-Packard") (HP), where he worked at the time, but was denied by the company on five occasions.[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-AI-19) Jobs eventually convinced Wozniak to go into business together and start a new company of their own.[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20045%E2%80%936-20) In order to raise the money they needed to produce the first batch of printed circuit boards, Jobs sold his [Volkswagen Type 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volkswagen_Type_2 "Volkswagen Type 2") [minibus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minibus "Minibus") for $1,500, and Wozniak his [HP-65](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HP-65 "HP-65") [programmable calculator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Programmable_calculator "Programmable calculator") for $500.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201562-18)[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20045-16)[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-21)[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-22)

### Apple I and company formation

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Apple_Garage.jpg/500px-Apple_Garage.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_Garage.jpg) [Steve Jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Jobs "Steve Jobs")' parents' home on Crist Drive in [Los Altos, California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Altos,_California "Los Altos, California"), where Apple started its operation.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048-23) Initial work took place in his bedroom and later moved to the home's garage.[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Apple_Confidential-15)[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Apple_1_Woz_1976_at_CHM.agr_cropped.jpg/500px-Apple_1_Woz_1976_at_CHM.agr_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_1_Woz_1976_at_CHM.agr_cropped.jpg) Wozniak's [Apple I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_I "Apple I") design was sold as an assembled circuit board and without any sort of covering. Users only needed to supply a TV and a keyboard.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Apple_first_logo.png/330px-Apple_first_logo.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_first_logo.png)

The first [Apple logo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_logo "Apple logo"), drawn by [Ronald Wayne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Wayne "Ronald Wayne"), depicts [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") under an apple tree.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Apple_Computer_Logo_rainbow.svg/330px-Apple_Computer_Logo_rainbow.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_Computer_Logo_rainbow.svg)

Created by [Rob Janoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rob_Janoff "Rob Janoff") in 1977, the Apple logo with the rainbow scheme was used from April of that year[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-24) until August 26, 1999.\[ _[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_\]

On April 1, 1976, Apple Computer Company was founded by Steve Jobs, Steve Wozniak, and [Ronald Wayne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Wayne "Ronald Wayne").[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-25)[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20045%E2%80%936-20) The company was registered as a California [business partnership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Business_partnership "Business partnership").[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201639-26)
Wayne, who worked at [Atari, Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atari,_Inc. "Atari, Inc.") as a chief draftsman, became a co-founder in return for a 10% stake.[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwaine2014336%E2%80%93338-27)[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20045%E2%80%936-20)[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-partnership-2) Wayne was gun-shy due to the failure of his own venture four years earlier. On April 12, less than two weeks after the company's formation, Wayne left Apple, selling his 10% share back to the two Steves for $800.[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201565%E2%80%9366-28)[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20046%E2%80%938-29)

According to Wozniak, Jobs proposed the name “Apple Computer” when he had just come back from [Robert Friedland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Friedland "Robert Friedland")'s All-One Farm in Oregon.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20046%E2%80%938-29) Jobs told [Walter Isaacson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Isaacson "Walter Isaacson") that he was "on one of my fruitarian diets," when he conceived of the name and thought "it sounded fun, spirited and not intimidating ... plus, it would get us ahead of Atari in the phone book."[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-30)

The two Steves made a last trip to the Homebrew Computer Club and demonstrated the [Apple I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_I "Apple I") (AKA: The Apple Computer).[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201639%E2%80%9340-31) [Paul Terrell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Terrell "Paul Terrell"), who operated the computer store chain [Byte Shop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byte_Shop "Byte Shop"), was impressed,[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwaine2014336%E2%80%93338-27) and gave the two Steves his card, asking them to keep in touch.[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201566-32) The next day, Jobs visited Terrell at the [Mountain View](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_View,_California "Mountain View, California") Byte Shop store, and tried to sell him the bare circuit boards for the Apple I.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20046%E2%80%938-29) Terrell said he was only interested in purchasing the machine fully assembled, and that he would order 50 assembled computers and pay US$500 each on delivery (equivalent to $2,800in 2025).[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-iCon-p35-33)[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20047-34)[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwaine2014336%E2%80%93338-27) Jobs took the purchase order from the Byte Shop to national electronic parts distributor Cramer Electronics, and ordered the components needed. When asked by the credit manager how he would pay for the parts, Jobs replied, "I have this purchase order from the Byte Shop chain of computer stores for 50 of my computers and the payment terms are [COD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cash_on_delivery "Cash on delivery"). If you give me the parts on net 30-day terms I can build and deliver the computers in that time frame, collect my money from Terrell at the Byte Shop and pay you."[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-35)[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-wozniak198412-36)

To verify the purchase order, the credit manager called Paul Terrell, who assured him if the computers showed up, Jobs would have more than enough money for the parts order. The two Steves and their small crew spent day and night building and testing the computers, and delivered to Terrell on time. Terrell was surprised to receive a batch of assembled circuit boards, as he had expected complete computers with a case, monitor and keyboard.[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201567%E2%80%9368-37)[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) Nonetheless, he kept his word and paid the two Steves the money promised.[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-VintageNews-39)[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201567%E2%80%9368-37)[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38)[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-iCon-p36-40)

The Apple I went on sale in July 1976 as an assembled circuit board with a retail price of $666.66.[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-41)[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20047%E2%80%938-42)[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201569%E2%80%9370-43) Wozniak later said he had had no idea about the relation between the number and the [mark of the beast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_of_the_beast "Mark of the beast"), and that he came up with the price because he liked repeating digits.[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-VintageNews-39) About 200 units of the Apple I were eventually sold.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200414-44)

The Apple I computer had some notable features, including the use of a [TV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Television "Television") display, whereas many machines had no display at all. This was not like the displays of later machines; the text was displayed at 60 characters per second – still faster than the teleprinters of contemporary machines of that era. The machine had [bootstrap](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Booting "Booting") code on [ROM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Read-only_memory "Read-only memory"), making it easier to start up. At the insistence of Paul Terrell, Wozniak designed a cassette interface for loading and saving programs, at the then-rapid pace of 1200bit/s. The simple machine was a masterpiece of design using far fewer parts than anything in its class, and earned Wozniak his reputation as a designer.

Jobs looked for investments to expand the business,[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) but banks were reluctant to lend him money; the idea of a computer for ordinary people seemed absurd at the time. In August 1976, Jobs approached his former boss at Atari, [Nolan Bushnell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nolan_Bushnell "Nolan Bushnell"), who recommended that he meet with [Don Valentine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don_Valentine "Don Valentine"), the founder of [Sequoia Capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequoia_Capital "Sequoia Capital").[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) Valentine was not interested in funding Apple, but in turn introduced Jobs to [Mike Markkula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Markkula "Mike Markkula"), a millionaire who had worked under him at [Fairchild Semiconductor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fairchild_Semiconductor "Fairchild Semiconductor").[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) Markkula saw great potential in the two Steves, and became an [angel investor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angel_investor "Angel investor") of their company.[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201645%E2%80%9347-45) He invested $92,000 in Apple out of his own property while securing a $250,000 (equivalent to $1,410,000in 2025) line of credit from Bank of America.[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201645%E2%80%9347-45)[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) In return, Markkula received a one-third stake in Apple.[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201645%E2%80%9347-45) Apple Computer, Inc. was incorporated on January 3, 1977.[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38) The new corporation bought out the partnership the two Steves had formed nine months earlier.[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201577%E2%80%9378-46)

In February 1977, Markkula recruited [Michael Scott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Scott_(Apple) "Michael Scott (Apple)") from [National Semiconductor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Semiconductor "National Semiconductor") to serve as the first president and CEO of Apple Computer, as the two Steves were both insufficiently experienced and he was not interested in taking that position himself.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200411-47)[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201648%E2%80%9350-48)
That same month, Wozniak resigned from his job at [Hewlett-Packard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hewlett-Packard "Hewlett-Packard") to work full-time for Apple.[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201577%E2%80%9378-46)[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200421-49)

### Apple II

Main article: [Apple II (original)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_II_(original) "Apple II (original)")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Apple_II_Plus_cropped.jpg/500px-Apple_II_Plus_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_II_Plus_cropped.jpg)

[Apple II Plus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_II_Plus "Apple II Plus"), designed primarily by Wozniak

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Disk_II.jpg/330px-Disk_II.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Disk_II.jpg)

[Disk II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disk_II "Disk II"), also designed by Wozniak

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/20863_Stevens_Creek_Boulevard.jpg/500px-20863_Stevens_Creek_Boulevard.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:20863_Stevens_Creek_Boulevard.jpg) Apple Computer's first headquarters office in Cupertino (photographed in 2025)

Almost as soon as Apple had started selling its first computers, Wozniak moved on from the Apple I and began designing a greatly improved computer: the [Apple II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_II_(original) "Apple II (original)").[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201645%E2%80%9347-45) Wozniak completed a working prototype of the new machine by August 1976.[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer20048%E2%80%9310-38)[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-50) The two Steves presented the Apple II computer to the public at the first [West Coast Computer Faire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Coast_Computer_Faire "West Coast Computer Faire") on April 16 and 17, 1977. In the May 1977 issue of [_Byte_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byte_(magazine) "Byte (magazine)"), Wozniak said of the Apple II design, "To me, a personal computer should be small, reliable, convenient to use, and inexpensive."[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-wozniak197705-51)

The Apple II went on sale on June 10, 1977, with a retail price of $1,298.[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-52) The computer's main internal difference from its predecessor was a completely redesigned TV interface, which held the display in memory. Now not only useful for simple text display, the Apple II included graphics and, eventually, color. During the development of the Apple II, Jobs pressed for a well-designed plastic case and built-in keyboard, with the idea that the machine should be fully packaged and ready to run out of the box.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201571%E2%80%9374-53) This was almost the case for the Apple I computers, but one still needed to plug various parts together and type in the code to run BASIC. Jobs wanted the Apple II case to be "simple and elegant", and hired an industrial designer named [Jerry Manock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerry_Manock "Jerry Manock") to produce such a case design.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201571%E2%80%9374-53) Apple employee #5 [Rod Holt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rod_Holt "Rod Holt") developed the [switching power supply](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Switched-mode_power_supply "Switched-mode power supply").[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-wozorgAppleII-54) While early Apple II models use ordinary cassette tapes as storage devices, they were superseded in 1978 by the introduction of a 5+1⁄4-inch [floppy disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floppy_disk "Floppy disk") drive and interface called the [Disk II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disk_II "Disk II").[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200413%E2%80%9315-55)[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-56) The Disk II system was designed by Wozniak and released with a retail price of $495.[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200413%E2%80%9315-55)

In 1979, the Apple II was chosen to be the desktop platform for what became the first [killer application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killer_application "Killer application") of the business world: [VisiCalc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VisiCalc "VisiCalc"), a [spreadsheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spreadsheet "Spreadsheet").[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200413%E2%80%9315-55) So important that the Apple II became what [John Markoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Markoff "John Markoff") described as a "VisiCalc accessory",[\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-markoff19820705-57) the application unexpectedly created a business market for the computer; Wozniak later said that small businesses bought 90% of Apple IIs, not the hobbyists he and Jobs had expected.[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-byte198501-58) It gave home users an additional reason to buy Apples: Compatibility with the office.[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200413%E2%80%9315-55) Before VisiCalc, Apple had been a distant third place competitor to [Commodore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodore_64 "Commodore 64") and [Tandy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tandy_Computers "Tandy Computers").[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-59)[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-60)

The Apple II was one of the three " [1977 Trinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_personal_computers#1977_and_the_emergence_of_the_%22Trinity%22 "History of personal computers")" computers generally credited with creating the [home computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home_computer "Home computer") market (the other two being the [Commodore PET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodore_PET "Commodore PET") and the Tandy Corporation [TRS-80](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TRS-80 "TRS-80")).[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-61) A number of different models of the Apple II were built thereafter, including the [Apple IIe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_IIe "Apple IIe") and [Apple IIGS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_IIGS "Apple IIGS"),[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200417%E2%80%9320-62) which continued in public use for nearly two decades. The [Apple II series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_II "Apple II") went on to sell about six million units in total before it was discontinued in 1993.[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201584%E2%80%9385-63)[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201665%E2%80%9366-64)

### Apple III

Main article: [Apple III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_III "Apple III")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Apple3.jpg/500px-Apple3.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple3.jpg) [Apple III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_III "Apple III") with [Apple Monitor III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Monitor_III "Apple Monitor III")

While the Apple II was already established as a successful business-ready platform because of VisiCalc, Apple management was not content. The Apple III was designed to take on the business environment in an attempt to compete with [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM") in the business and corporate computing market.[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady20096-65) The development of the Apple III started in late 1978 under the guidance of Wendell Sander,[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200481%E2%80%9382-66) and was subsequently developed by a committee headed by Jobs.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200441%E2%80%9342-67) The Apple III was first announced on May 19, 1980, with a retail price ranging from $4,340
to $7,800, and released in November 1980.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200441%E2%80%9342-67)

The Apple III was a conservative design for the era, however Jobs wanted the heat generated by the electronics to be dissipated through the [chassis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chassis "Chassis") of the machine rather than by the more usual cooling fan. The case was not sufficient to cool the components and the Apple III was prone to overheating, causing the [integrated circuit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_circuit "Integrated circuit") chips to disconnect from the motherboard. Customers who contacted Apple customer service were told to raise their computers into the air, and then let go to cause the integrated circuits to fall back into place. Thousands of Apple III computers were recalled. A new model was introduced in 1983 to try to rectify the problems, but the damage was already done.

### Apple IPO

In the July 1980 issue of _[Kilobaud Microcomputing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilobaud_Microcomputing "Kilobaud Microcomputing")_, publisher [Wayne Green](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayne_Green "Wayne Green") stated that "the best consumer ads I've seen have been those by Apple. They are attention-getting, and they must be prompting sale."[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-green198007-68) In August, the _[Financial Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Financial_Times "Financial Times")_ reported that

> Apple Computer, the fast growing Californian manufacturer of small computers for the consumer, business and educational markets, is planning to go public later this year. \[It\] is the largest private manufacturer in the U.S. of small computers. Founded about five years ago as a small workshop business, it has become the second largest manufacturer of small computers, after the [Radio Shack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_Shack "Radio Shack") division of the [Tandy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tandy_Corporation "Tandy Corporation") company.[\[69\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-69)

On December 12, 1980, Apple went public on the [NASDAQ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASDAQ "NASDAQ") stock exchange with the ticker symbol "AAPL", selling 4.6million shares at $22 per share ($.10 per share when adjusting for [stock splits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock_split "Stock split") as of November30,2020[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Apple_Inc.&action=edit)),[\[70\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Apple_FAQ-70) generating over $100million, which was more capital than any IPO since [Ford Motor Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company "Ford Motor Company") in 1956.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-EDNAAPLSTOCK-71) Several venture capitalists cashed out, reaping billions in long-term capital gains. By the end of the day, the stock rose to $29 per share and 300millionaires were created, including the two Steves.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-CNBC-72)[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-DEDIPO-73) Around this time Wozniak offered $10million of his own stock to early Apple employees, something Jobs refused to do.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-CNBC-72) Apple's market cap was $1.778billion at the end of its first day of trading.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-EDNAAPLSTOCK-71)[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-DEDIPO-73)

In January 1981, Apple held its first shareholders meeting as a public company in the [Flint Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flint_Center "Flint Center"), a large auditorium at nearby [De Anza College](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Anza_College "De Anza College") (which is often used for symphony concerts) to handle the larger numbers of shareholders post-IPO. The business of the meeting had been planned so that the voting could be staged in 15 minutes or less. In most cases, voting proxies are collected by mail and counted days or months before a meeting. In this case, after the IPO, many shares were in new hands.

Jobs started his prepared speech, but after being interrupted by voting several times, he dropped his prepared speech and delivered a long, emotionally charged talk about betrayal, lack of respect, and related topics.[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-74) The results of the vote were surprising: a young programmer, [Randy Wigginton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Randy_Wigginton "Randy Wigginton"), received enough votes to be added to Apple's board of directors, through the use of [cumulative voting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cumulative_voting "Cumulative voting") by a few major shareholders.

### Competition from the IBM PC

By August 1981 Apple was among the three largest microcomputer companies, perhaps having replaced Radio Shack as the leader;[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-hogan19810831-75) revenue in the first half of the year had already exceeded 1980's $118 million, and _InfoWorld_ reported that lack of production capacity was constraining growth.[\[76\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-hogan19810914state-76) Because of VisiCalc, businesses purchased 90% of Apple II computers;[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Mace1984-77)[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-byte198501-58) large customers especially preferred Apple.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-mcmullen19840221-78)

[IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM") entered the personal computer market that month with the [IBM PC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_Personal_Computer "IBM Personal Computer")[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-pollack19810813-79)[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-isaacson2013-80) in part because it did not want products without IBM logos on customers' desks,[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-pollack19830327-81) but Apple had many advantages. While IBM began with one microcomputer, little available hardware or software, and a couple of hundred dealers, Apple had five times as many dealers in the US and an established international distribution network. The Apple II had an [installed base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Installed_base "Installed base") of more than 250,000 customers, and hundreds of independent developers offered software and peripherals; at least ten databases and ten word processors were available, while the PC had no databases and one word processor.[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-rosen19811130-82)

The company's customers gained a reputation for devotion and loyalty. _[BYTE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BYTE "BYTE")_ in 1984 stated that[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-lemmons198412-83)

> There are two kinds of people in the world: people who say Apple isn't just a company, it's a cause; and people who say Apple isn't a cause, it's just a company. Both groups are right. Nature has suspended the principle of noncontradiction where Apple is concerned.
>
> Apple is more than just a company because its founding has some of the qualities of myth ... Apple is two guys in a garage undertaking the mission of bringing computing power, once reserved for big corporations, to ordinary individuals with ordinary budgets. The company's growth from two guys to a billion-dollar corporation exemplifies the [American Dream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Dream "American Dream"). Even as a large corporation, Apple plays [David to IBM's Goliath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_and_Goliath "David and Goliath"), and thus has the sympathetic role in that myth.

The magazine noted that the loyalty was not entirely positive for Apple; customers were willing to overlook real flaws in its products, even while holding the company to a higher standard than for competitors.[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-lemmons198412-83) The Apple III was an example of its autocratic reputation among dealers[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-mcmullen19840221-78) that one described as "Apple arrogance".[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-dvorak19831128-84) After examining a PC and finding it unimpressive, Apple confidently purchased a full-page advertisement in _The Wall Street Journal_ with the headline "Welcome, IBM. Seriously".[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-iw19811005-85)[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-isaacson2013-80) The company prioritized the III for three years, spending what Wozniak estimated as $100 million on marketing and R&D while not improving the Apple II to compete with the PC, as doing so could hurt III sales.[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-byte198501-58)

Microsoft head [Bill Gates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Gates "Bill Gates") was at Apple headquarters the day of IBM's announcement and later said "They didn't seem to care. It took them a full year to realize what had happened".[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-isaacson2013-80) The PC almost completely ended sales of the III, the company's most comparable product. The II still sold well,[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-pollack19830327-81) with Apple being the leading computer manufacturer in the United States where 7 million units were sold between 1978 and 1982.[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-86) But by 1983, the PC surpassed the Apple II as the best-selling personal computer.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-sanger19850805-87) IBM recruited the best Apple dealers while avoiding the discount [grey market](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grey_market#Electronics "Grey market") they disliked.[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-pollack19830327-81) The head of a retail chain said "It appears that IBM had a better understanding of why the Apple II was successful than had Apple".[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-mcmullen19840221-78) [Gene Amdahl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_Amdahl "Gene Amdahl") predicted that Apple would be another of the many "brash young companies" that IBM had defeated.[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-greenwald19830711-88)

By 1984 the press called the two companies archrivals,[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-bulman19841105-89) but IBM had $4 billion in annual PC revenue, more than twice that of Apple and as much as the sales of it and the next three companies combined.[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-libes198509-90) A _[Fortune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fortune_(magazine) "Fortune (magazine)")_ survey found that 56% of American companies with personal computers used IBM PCs, compared to 16% for Apple.[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Kennedy_1985-91) Small businesses, schools, and some homes became the II's primary market.[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Mace1984-77)

### Xerox PARC and the Lisa

Main article: [Apple Lisa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Lisa "Apple Lisa")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Apple_Lisa-2-IMG_1730_modified.png/500px-Apple_Lisa-2-IMG_1730_modified.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apple_Lisa-2-IMG_1730_modified.png) [Apple Lisa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Lisa "Apple Lisa")

In 1979, in return for the right to purchase $1 million worth of Apple’s pre-IPO stock, [Xerox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xerox "Xerox") granted Apple access to its [Palo Alto Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARC_(company) "PARC (company)") (PARC).[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady20097%E2%80%938-92)[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200473%E2%80%9376-93) Jobs, along with his Apple colleagues, visited PARC twice in November and December of 1979,[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200474%E2%80%9375-94)[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-95)[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Wired-96)[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Jobsjourney-97) and he was greatly impressed by the advanced [graphical user interface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphical_user_interface "Graphical user interface") (GUI) of the [Xerox Alto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xerox_Alto "Xerox Alto") computer.[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady20097%E2%80%938-92)[\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson201596%E2%80%9397-98)

After the visits to PARC, Jobs decided to implement a GUI like the one he had seen there in the [Apple Lisa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Lisa "Apple Lisa"), a business-oriented computer that had been under development at Apple since July 1979.[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200473%E2%80%9376-93)[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201681%E2%80%9389-99) Jobs took the lead of the Lisa project from early 1980, but the development of the Lisa was disrupted due to his excessive technical demands.[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201681%E2%80%9389-99) In September 1980, Jobs was removed from the Lisa project.[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015110-100)

The first iteration of Apple's [WIMP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WIMP_(computing) "WIMP (computing)") interface was a floppy disk where files could be spatially moved around. After months of usability testing, Apple designed the Lisa interface of windows and icons.

Finally released in January 1983, the Lisa featured many innovative functions such as a GUI and a [mouse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_mouse "Computer mouse"), but its extremely high price of US$9,995 (equivalent to $32,300in 2025) and the lack of software compatibility drove business users away, resulting in sluggish sales.[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200468-101) More than $150 million was reportedly spent on the development of the Lisa, but only about 10,000 units were sold, resulting in significant losses for Apple.[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Grady200972-102)

### Macintosh and the "1984" commercial

Main articles: [History of the Macintosh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Macintosh "History of the Macintosh") and [1984 (advertisement)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1984_(advertisement) "1984 (advertisement)")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Macintosh%2C_Google_NY_office_computer_museum_cropped.png/500px-Macintosh%2C_Google_NY_office_computer_museum_cropped.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Macintosh,_Google_NY_office_computer_museum_cropped.png) [Macintosh 128K](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macintosh_128K "Macintosh 128K")

The Macintosh project was started in September 1979 by [Jeff Raskin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeff_Raskin "Jeff Raskin"), an early Apple employee and a computer scientist.[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELInzmayer200487%E2%80%9388-103)[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-104) The [Macintosh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mac_(computer) "Mac (computer)"), named after Raskin’s favorite [type of apple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McIntosh_(apple) "McIntosh (apple)"), was originally conceived as a low-cost [portable computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portable_computer "Portable computer").[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwaine2000308-105)[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELInzmayer200487-106) The project entered a new phase when Steve Jobs, having been removed from the Lisa project, joined the team in January 1981.[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELInzmayer200492-107)[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015109%E2%80%93113-108) Jobs soon took over the project from Raskin to redefine the Macintosh as a high-performance desktop machine similar to the Lisa, featuring a [mouse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_mouse "Computer mouse")-driven [graphical user interface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphical_user_interface "Graphical user interface") and powered by the [Motorola 68000](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motorola_68000 "Motorola 68000") microprocessor.[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015109%E2%80%93113,_136-109) Wozniak, who led the initial design and development with Raskin was on leave during this time due to an airplane crash, making it easier for Jobs to seize control of the program.[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-TheVerge-110)[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-wozorg-111)

By 1984 computer dealers saw Apple as the only clear alternative to IBM's influence;[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-sanger19841119-112) some even promoted its products to reduce dependence on the PC.[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-pollack19830327-81) The company announced the [Macintosh 128K](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macintosh_128K "Macintosh 128K") to the press in October 1983, followed by an 18-page brochure included with magazines in December.[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-113) Its debut was announced by a single national broadcast of a US$1.5 million television commercial, " [1984](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1984_(advertisement) "1984 (advertisement)")" (equivalent to $4,600,000in 2025). Directed by [Ridley Scott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ridley_Scott "Ridley Scott") and aired during the third quarter of [Super Bowl XVIII](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Super_Bowl_XVIII "Super Bowl XVIII") on January 22, 1984,[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-114) it is considered a "watershed event"[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-115) and a "masterpiece."[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-masterpiece-116) The commercial alludes to [George Orwell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Orwell "George Orwell")'s novel _[Nineteen Eighty-Four](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four "Nineteen Eighty-Four")_ which describes a [dystopian future](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dystopia "Dystopia") of enforced conformity. In the commercial a heroine represents the coming of the Macintosh to save humanity,[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-cellini-117) and ends with the words: "On January 24th, Apple Computer will introduce Macintosh. And you'll see why 1984 won't be like 1984.”[\[118\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer2004110%E2%80%93113-118)

On January 24, 1984, the Macintosh went on sale with a retail price of $2,495.[\[119\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-119)[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200491-120) It came bundled with two applications designed to show off its interface: [MacWrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MacWrite "MacWrite") and [MacPaint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MacPaint "MacPaint"). On the same day, an emotional Jobs introduced the computer to a wildly enthusiastic audience at Apple's annual shareholders meeting held in the Flint Auditorium;[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015167%E2%80%93170-121)[\[122\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201682%E2%80%9383-122) Macintosh engineer [Andy Hertzfeld](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andy_Hertzfeld "Andy Hertzfeld") described the scene as "pandemonium".[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-The_Times_They_Are_A-Changin-123) The Macintosh was widely acclaimed by the media with strong initial sales supporting it.[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015185%E2%80%93187-124)[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201684%E2%80%9388-125) However, the slow processing speed and limited software led to a rapid sales decline in the second half of 1984.[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2015185%E2%80%93187-124)[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlender201684%E2%80%9388-125)[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinzmayer200498-126)

The Macintosh was too radical for some, who labeled it a mere "toy". Because the machine was entirely designed around the GUI, existing text-mode and [command-driven applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Command_line_interface "Command line interface") had to be redesigned and the programming code rewritten; this was a challenging undertaking that many [software developers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_developer "Software developer") shied away from, and resulted in an initial lack of software for the new system. In April 1984 [Microsoft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft "Microsoft")'s [Multiplan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplan "Multiplan") migrated over from [MS-DOS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MS-DOS "MS-DOS"), followed by [Microsoft Word](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft_Word "Microsoft Word") in January 1985;[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-hotwyu-127) in return for Microsoft's commitment to Macintosh, Apple agreed to not provide [software development kits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_development_kit "Software development kit") and prototype computers to companies with applications competing with Microsoft's.[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-farber20140123-128) In 1985, [Lotus Development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lotus_Development "Lotus Development") introduced [Lotus Jazz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lotus_Jazz "Lotus Jazz") after the success of [Lotus 1-2-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lotus_1-2-3 "Lotus 1-2-3") for the IBM PC, although it was largely a flop.[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-129) Apple introduced [Macintosh Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macintosh_Office "Macintosh Office") the same year with the [lemmings ad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemmings_(advertisement) "Lemmings (advertisement)"), infamous for insulting potential customers. It was not successful.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Worst_Business-130)

For a special post-election edition of _[Newsweek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newsweek "Newsweek")_ in November 1984, Apple spent more than US$2.5 million to buy all 39 of the advertising pages in the issue.[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-131) Apple also ran a "Test Drive a Macintosh" promotion, in which potential buyers with a credit card could take home a Macintosh for 24 hours and return it to a dealer afterwards. While 200,000 people participated, dealers disliked the promotion, the supply of computers was insufficient for demand, and many were returned in such a bad shape that they could no longer be sold. This marketing campaign caused CEO [John Sculley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Sculley "John Sculley") to raise the price from US$1,995 (equivalent to $6,200in 2025) to US$2,495 (equivalent to $7,700in 2025).[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc.#cite_note-Worst_Business-130)

### Jobs and Wozniak leave Apple
